    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8551901e12544886ac7b08a870782d7b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '46d1ba32-f8f9-4e80-828f-5c9501df15ae'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d17793171" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Niobium oxides have fascinated numerous interests for ultrafast sodium-ion batteries due to their impressive stability, fast pseudocapacitive kinetics, and high safety features. However, the poor intrinsic electronic conductivity and unrevealed mechanisms largely limit their further applications. Here, inspired by density functional theory (DFT) calculations, the introduction of conductor heterostructure (Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub>) can largely decrease the bulk phase inserted energy of Na<sup>+</sup> from 9.88 to â3.54&nbsp;eV, as well as promote the delocalization of electron, greatly improving the electrical conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. As expected, the as-obtained orthorhombic niobium pentoxide (T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C) delivers a high conductivity of 3.69&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>, exhibiting a great improvement of four orders magnitude than that of the parent Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (1.87&nbsp;Ã&nbsp;10<sup>â4</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>). Impressively, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C possesses a high reversible capacity of 249&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, as well as excellent rate performance of 63&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, nearly twins the capacity of parent porous Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Furthermore, the periodic evolutions of the main peaks for targeted electrodes during repeated charging/discharging processes have been further revealed by in situ XRD, demonstrating a highly reversible inserted/extracted storage mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>. This work provides a novel method to improve the conductivity of materials by constructing conductor heterojunction.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202312905-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the recent two decades, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have mainly dominated the portable electronics applications, 3C consumer electronics, electric vehicles, and hybrid electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0001_R_d17793163e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature Small">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have appealed numerous attention as cheaper alternatives to LIBs for large-scale renewable energy storage and smart grid application by the virtue of their low cost associated with rich resources of sodium and relatively high energy densities compared with other energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0002_R_d17793163e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the practical application of SIBs in large scale energy storage fields is also hindered by the relatively low reversible capacity and poor rate capability, which can be attributed to the large volume changes resulting from the repeated insertion/extraction processes of large Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0003_R_d17793163e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater. Green Chem. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The application and development of high-performance SIBs lie in the electrodes, which makes the exploration for long cycling life and outstanding rate capability electrode materials has been the most attractive field for the SIBs. For anode materials of SIBs, hard carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0004_R_d17793163e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Mater. Today Small Adv. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> soft carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0005_R_d17793163e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Alloys Compd. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal/alloy,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0006_R_d17793163e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small Methods Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition metal oxide,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0007_R_d17793163e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett. Energy Storage Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Sci. Bull.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition metal sulfide,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0008_R_d17793163e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and transition metal selenide,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0009_R_d17793163e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small Adv. Mater. ACS Nano">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been extensively explored. The orthorhombic niobium pentoxide (T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) has been regarded as promising anode materials by reason of their unique layered structure, high chemical stability, and open intrinsic framework, which are typical pseudocapacitive materials and can store energy through fast surface redox reactions, delivering the prospect of high power delivery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0002_R_d17793163e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0010_R_d17793163e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, by utilizing Niobium oxides as an anode, issues about electrolyte instability and lithium (sodium) dendrite formation below 1.0&nbsp;V versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) in anode materials can be efficiently avoided, making batteries much safer compared to the commercial graphite and hard carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0011_R_d17793163e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that the density of niobium oxides is generally greater than 4.0&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, so its volume-specific capacity is much higher than its mass-specific capacity, which will have a wide range of applications in the field with high volumetric energy density requirements.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0012_R_d17793163e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the Niobium oxides are non-combustible, in extreme conditions, such as violent collision, fire, etc., will have more excellent safety performance. Based on this, there is a lot of researches focused on the performance improvement of Niobium oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0010_R_d17793163e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reports on Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> for sodium storage are relatively less, which may be attributed to its poor electrochemical performance resulting from the low electronic conductivity (â3&nbsp;Ã&nbsp;10<sup>â6</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>) and the sluggish diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0011_R_d17793163e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Adv. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0014_R_d17793163e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, numerous efforts have been devoted to developing various approaches to modify the electronic conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> to enhance its electrochemical performance. Designing various nanostructures are efficient method to improve the electrochemical performance of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which can largely reduce the diffusion distances of both ions and electrons during the charge/discharge processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0015_R_d17793163e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chemistry">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, integrating the carbon coating method with various Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanostructures to fabricate composite materials also can enhance the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0016_R_d17793163e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, carbon-confined Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanoparticles were designed by Mai et&nbsp;al. via a mismatched coordination reaction, which delivered high reversible capacity and rapid rate capability for lithium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> He and co-workers developed an N, S co-doped carbon coating approach to enhance the electrochemical performance of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> by using a convenient thiourea evaporation method, delivering excellent structural reversibility and good rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0014_R_d17793163e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, Yu and co-workers reported a sulfur-doped Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> material by using the hard template method, in which the incorporation of the sulfur can produce a considerable amount of oxygen defects, narrowing the bandgap, and enhancing the conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0002_R_d17793163e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0017_R_d17793163e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The as-obtained S-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-HNS@S-rGO material exhibits a high reversible capacity 215&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.5 C over 100 cycles. These are conventional methods to improve the conductivity of electrode materials, it is very important to develop new methods to improve the electrochemical properties of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Inspired by our previous approach to improve the electrochemical performance of electrode materials by constructing heterogeneous interfaces and excellent electronic conductivity of NbSe<sub>2</sub>, a new strategy for constructing conductor heterojunctions has emerged.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, the conductor heterostructure Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub> material was successfully designed by an in situ selenization process, which largely decreased the bulk phase inserted energy of Na<sup>+</sup> from 9.88 to â3.54&nbsp;eV according to the DFT simulations, as well as promoted the delocalization of electron, greatly improving the electrical conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> from 1.87&nbsp;Ã&nbsp;10<sup>â4</sup> to 3.69&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>. Remarkably, the as-fabricated T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C delivers an impressively reversible capacity of 249&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, and excellent rate performance of 63&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, nearly twins the capacity of porous Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, 33&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>). Furthermore, the slight and repeated movements of the main peaks for the targeted electrode during repeated charging/discharging processes have been further discovered by in situ XRD, signifying a highly reversible inserted/extracted storage mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>. This work provides a unique method to improve the conductivity of materials by constructing conductor heterojunction, which may be expanded to other electrodes to enhance the electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202312905-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202312905-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>To better recognize the energy storage advantages of Nb-based heterogeneous structures, the first-principles calculation method based on density functional theory (DFT) was utilized to analyze the potential energy performance of sodium ions. The side view and top view crystal structures of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, NbSe<sub>2</sub>, and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1aâc</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1aâc</a> (Supporting Information). To examine the effects of heterostructure on the electronic structures, their energy bandgaps and density of states (DOS) are also calculated. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information), the energy bandgaps of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is 1.486&nbsp;eV, indicating the poor electronic conductivity. While, the energy bandgaps of NbSe<sub>2</sub> is 0.0&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information), exhibiting metal-like properties and good electronic conductivity, which is similar to the previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0018_R_d17793163e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the introduction of the heterostructure, the energy bandgaps of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub> is 0&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information), delivering largely enhanced electronic conductivity compared with Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Besides, as presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a> (Supporting Information), the energy bandgaps Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> also can be decreased after the introduction of the oxygen defect.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4064c821-5219-4915-af1a-aeedfd24565d/adfm202312905-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4064c821-5219-4915-af1a-aeedfd24565d/adfm202312905-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0e9b61c-c750-46d1-85e6-e81f7308b258/adfm202312905-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4064c821-5219-4915-af1a-aeedfd24565d/adfm202312905-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic crystal structure of the materials: a) Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, b) NbSe<sub>2</sub>, and c) Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The calculated partial density of states for d) Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, e) NbSe<sub>2</sub>, and f) Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub>, respectively. g,h) Charge density difference of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub>. i) The adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> on the different active sites.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, valence band maximum (VBM) near the Fermi energy level for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1d</a>), and NbSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1e</a>) is mainly contributed by p orbitals of oxygen/selenium and conduction band minimum (CBM) is primarily dominated by 4d orbitals of Nb. The 2p orbitals peak intensity of VBM in Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is relatively high, indicating the strong localization of electrons, which reflects the poor electronic conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. While the 3p orbitals peak intensity of VBM in NbSe<sub>2</sub> is relatively low and broad, indicating the good delocalization and mobility of electron. In Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1f</a>), both of the p orbitals of VBM peak and 4d orbitals in CBM are relatively low and broad, manifesting that the excess electrons can easily transfer from p orbitals to metal element 4d orbitals, which results in a decrease in their bandgaps. As presented in the charge density difference of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1g,h</a>), the electron transfer phenomenon from NbSe<sub>2</sub> to Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> can be found, which largely increases the electron delocalization energy and improves the conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Besides, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a> (Supporting Information), the oxygen defects can decrease the energy level of the CBM, and thus reduce the bandgaps of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Based on this, the sodium storage properties of related materials on different active sites are further investigated (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0001">1i</a>, the adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> on the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> bulk phase, NbSe<sub>2</sub> bulk phase, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub> bulk phase, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/NbSe<sub>2</sub>, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (001) surface, and NbSe<sub>2</sub> (001) surface are 9.88, â2.13, 8.73, â3.54, â2.75, and â1.66&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating that by introducing conductor heterostructure and defects to change the electron energy level structure can significantly improve the electrochemical performance of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Theoretical calculations confirm that the construction of transition Niobium conductor heterostructure can meet the demand for anode materials in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202312905-sec-0040-title">2.2 Material Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C composites is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0002">2</a>. The reducing properties of the carbon dots (CDs) were utilized to partially reduce pentavalent niobium under an argon atmosphere, thereby modulating the electronic and crystal structures of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and ultimately enhancing its bulk electronic and ionic conductivities. As listed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information), the broad peak of the carbon dots is located at 17Â°, which is significantly different compared to the graphitized carbon, indicating that the obtained dots exhibit a hybrid polymer-carbon structure and deliver various functional groups. Besides, according to Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> (Supporting Information), it is easily observed that the synthesized carbon dots are uniformly distributed with a size concentration of 2â5&nbsp;nm, which predicts that carbon dots as a novel carbon source can achieve a uniform coating of the material. The Nb-CDs precursors were synthesized by a simple solvothermal method. The microscopic morphology of the Nb-CDs precursor is demonstrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3a,e</a>. As can be seen, the Nb-CDs precursors appear as spherical particles with diameters of 1â2&nbsp;Âµm and their surfaces are very smooth. The uniform distribution of the Nb-CDs precursor particles, with no exceptionally small particles, suggests that the reaction time is sufficient, which is consistent with the âOstwald ripening mechanismâ of crystal growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0019_R_d17793163e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By calcining the precursor under high-purity argon, it was transformed into P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which maintains the overall morphology of micrometer-spherical particles but has a roughened surface compared to the Nb-CDs precursor (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3b,f</a>). Next, the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> microsphere particles were calcined in hydrogen/argon (5%/95%) mixture gas at 600&nbsp;Â°C for 4&nbsp;h to introduce oxygen vacancies to obtain T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3c,g</a>). The introduction of oxygen vacancies can modulate the electronic and crystal structure, which will facilitate electron and ion transport. Finally, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C composites can be obtained by solemnization using selenium powder under a hydrogen-argon mixture. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3d,h</a>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C still maintains the morphology of micron spherical particles. Meanwhile, the particle size is also concentrated at 1â2&nbsp;Âµm, and the particle size is basically unchanged and more uniformly distributed.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c61c8d14-d009-4e1c-a60e-a5df7a05e886/adfm202312905-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c61c8d14-d009-4e1c-a60e-a5df7a05e886/adfm202312905-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6ccd9de-7935-4de9-8b55-dce33277aed9/adfm202312905-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c61c8d14-d009-4e1c-a60e-a5df7a05e886/adfm202312905-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The synthesis process for the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/61b3380e-928f-4f5a-85d0-54a9ab8d9cfc/adfm202312905-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/61b3380e-928f-4f5a-85d0-54a9ab8d9cfc/adfm202312905-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88bfe54b-334c-4014-a0a0-9eb1c9d3451e/adfm202312905-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/61b3380e-928f-4f5a-85d0-54a9ab8d9cfc/adfm202312905-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The SEM images of Nb-CDs Complex a,e), P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> b,f), T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C c,g), and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C d,h). iâk)TEM and l) HRTEM images of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C; (m-q)TEM image and corresponding EDX element mapping image of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>TEM characterization was employed to further characterize the microstructure of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C. As can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3i-k</a>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C exhibits a micrometer spherical morphology, which is consistent with the SEM results. It can also be found that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub> particles are tightly encapsulated by the carbon layer, which is more similar to the structure of pomegranate. From the HRTEM of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3l</a>, it can be noticed that the crystallographic spacing of the material is 0.243&nbsp;nm, which corresponds to the (201) crystallographic plane of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. In addition, the results of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3l</a> also indicate the uniform encapsulation of the carbon layer as well as the thickness of the carbon layer of â8&nbsp;nm. The elemental distribution of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C is given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0003">3mâq</a>, which clearly indicates the uniform distribution of the elements C, Se, O, and Nb, also indicating the successful introduction of the Se element.</p>
            
            <p>The XRD results of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4a</a>. All of them present very sharp diffraction peaks, indicating that the materials are well crystallized. It also can be found that all three samples contain two phases, and the positions of the diffraction peaks of the main phases of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C are in agreement with those of the standard card (JCPDS No. 27â1003), which suggests that the main phase is indeed T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. In addition, there is a small peak near 26Â°, which originated from NbO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 74â1378), suggesting that a part of the Nb<sup>5+</sup> has been reduced. It is also predicted that P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C contain oxygen vacancies. Compared with P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, the small spike located near 26Â° in T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C is sharper, which may indicate that T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C contains more oxygen vacancies. In addition, it can be clearly seen that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample consists of two phases, and the position of the diffraction peak in the main phase is consistent with that of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> standard card (JCPDS No. 27â1003). Compared with P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample displays no sharp peak near 26Â°, but there is a clear sharp peak near 14Â°, which originates from NbSe<sub>2</sub>(JCPDS No. 72â1621). This indicates that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample does not contain the material phase NbO<sub>2</sub>, but contains the phase of NbSe<sub>2</sub>. In order to further analyze the physical phases in P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C crystal structure and physical phase content, we refined the XRD data using the Rietveld method (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4bâd</a>), and the obtained results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). It can be found that for the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples, the full-spectrum factor R<sub>p</sub> and the weighted full-spectrum factor R<sub>wp</sub> are both less than 10% when the structural models of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and NbO<sub>2</sub> are used for the fitting, indicating that the fitting results are good. Compared with the standard cards, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and NbO<sub>2</sub> in the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples differ only slightly in cell size. More importantly, the content of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> in both samples exceeds 98.5%, indicating a predominance of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. In contrast with the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample containing 0.88% of NbO<sub>2</sub>, the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C sample contained 1.08% of NbO<sub>2</sub>, indicating that more Nb<sup>5+</sup> was reduced in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C sample, which can produce more oxygen vacancies. For the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples, the structural models of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and NbSe<sub>2</sub> were fitted using the full-spectrum factor R<sub>p</sub> and the weighted full-spectrum factor R<sub>wp</sub> of 7.34% and 10.01%, respectively, which demonstrated that the fitting results were also good. The T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and NbSe<sub>2</sub> in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples are only slightly different in terms of cell size when compared to the standard cards. Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) lists the content of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and NbSe<sub>2</sub> phases in T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples as 78.81% and 21.16%, respectively, suggesting that NbSe<sub>2</sub> will have a greater influence on the properties of the samples.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fb6ff53d-ebd1-43f6-8201-8f8028412dd1/adfm202312905-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fb6ff53d-ebd1-43f6-8201-8f8028412dd1/adfm202312905-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61272176-eb6a-4c06-84f2-72ee9fab45a0/adfm202312905-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fb6ff53d-ebd1-43f6-8201-8f8028412dd1/adfm202312905-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The XRD patterns of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C; bâd) Rietveld refinement patterns of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C. EPRe), Raman spectra f,g), Carbon contents h) and electrical conductivity(i) of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The oxygen vacancies in P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C can be detected by electron paramagnetic spectroscopy (EPR).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4e</a>, P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C all exhibited obvious signals, indicating the presence of oxygen vacancies in the three samples. It is noted that the signal intensity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C is slightly lower than that of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which may be due to partial oxidation. Compared to the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample, the slightly lower and slightly off-centered signal intensity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C may be attributed to the doping of the Se element. As the Raman spectroscopy shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4f</a>, there are two distinct peaks located at 1330 and 1596&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup> of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples, which can be attributed to the D-band due to sp<sup>3</sup>-type amorphous carbon and the G-band originated from to sp<sup>2</sup>-type ordered graphitic carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0020_R_d17793163e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity ratio of the G band to the D band (I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D</sub>) of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C sample is 1.15, which is higher than that of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> of 1.14. This suggests that there are fewer defects and disordered structures in the carbon of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C sample, which may be thermally reduced by hydrogen. Unlike P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, the I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D</sub> of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample is 1.12. This indicates the presence of more defects and disordered structure of the carbon on its surface, which may be due to the doping of the Se element. In addition, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4g</a>, it can be seen that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples present four relatively obvious vibrational peaks below 700&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>, relating to the vibration of NbâO bonds, which is also consistent with the results reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0013_R_d17793163e1615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noted that the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample delivers four weaker vibrational peaks below 700&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>, which may be related to the higher carbon content of the sample surface. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4h</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the C contents in the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples were 8.55 wt.% and 8.57 wt.%, respectively, revealing that the C contents in the two samples were the same. Unlike the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples, the C content of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C was 7.5 wt.%, which turned out to be lower than the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples. This further indicates the successful doping of elemental Se in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample, which is consistent with the TEM and XRD results. In order to illustrate the effect of introducing oxygen vacancies and Se elemental doping on the electronic conductivity of the three samples, we analyzed the electronic conductivity of the three samples using a four-probe conductivity tester. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0004">4i</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the electrical conductivity of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample is very poor at 1.87&nbsp;Ã&nbsp;10<sup>â4</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>, whereas the electronic conductivity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C increases to 1.59&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>. This suggests that the introduction of oxygen vacancies can significantly improve the bulk phase conductivity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, probably due to the fact that it has a significant effect on the electronic structure of the material. The electronic conductivity of the composites was further increased to 3.69&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup> after the introduction of Se elements into T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C to form T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C. This suggests that the nonmetallic Se doping was able to increase the bulk phase electronic conductivity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> even further, the reason may be that Se doping produced NbSe<sub>2</sub> in the conductor phase which affects the electronic structure of the composites.</p>
            
            <p>In order to investigate the composition and chemical state of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C, XPS analysis was performed. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5a</a>, the total spectrum of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C shows the presence of C, O, Se, and Nb elements, which proves the successful doping of the Se element. The C1s high-resolution spectrum of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C shows that it can be divided into four fitted peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5b</a>). The C-C peak at 284.8&nbsp;eV is relatively strong, while the CâO and OâCâO peaks at 286.7 and 288.5&nbsp;eV are very weak, which suggests that the oxygen content in the carbon layer on the surface of the sample is relatively low. The high-resolution spectra of O 1s shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5c</a> can be decomposed into three peaks, including the NbâO bond at 530.8&nbsp;eV, the CâO bond at 532.0&nbsp;eV, and the CâO bond at 533.5&nbsp;eV, respectively. The high-resolution spectra of the Nb3d in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C sample are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5d</a>. The 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 207.8&nbsp;eV and the 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 210.5&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of Nb<sup>5+</sup>, while the 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 206.3&nbsp;eV and the 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 209.1&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of Nb<sup>4+</sup>. Nb<sup>4+</sup> exhibits a 4d-orbital electron compared to Nb<sup>5+</sup>, which favors the electronic conductivity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C. The high-resolution spectra of Se3d and Nb4s for the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples are displayed respectively in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5e</a>. It can be decomposed into 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 53.6&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 54.40&nbsp;eV (SeâNb bond), 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 55.4&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 56.23&nbsp;eV (SeâSe bond), and 3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 56.3&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 57.9&nbsp;eV (SeâC bond), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0021_R_d17793163e1818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of SeâNb bonds and Se-C proves that Se elements are not only doped into the surface carbon layer but also into the lattice of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which is consistent with the results of TEM and XRD. As a comparison (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0005">5f</a>), the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples contain only Nb4s peaks but not Se3d peaks. This reinforces the presence of Se in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C samples. As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information), the total spectra of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples indicate the presence of elemental C, O, and Nb. The high-resolution spectra of C 1s in P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> (Supporting Information) show that they can both be divided into three fitted peaks, the CâC bond located at 284.8&nbsp;eV, and the CâO and OâCâO bonds located at 286.7 and 288.5&nbsp;eV, respectively. The intensity of the CâC bond at 284.8&nbsp;eV is larger compared to the intensities of the peaks for the CâO and OâCâO bonds, which suggests that the oxygen content in the carbon layer on the surface of both samples is relatively low. As exhibited in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a> (Supporting Information), the O 1s resolution spectra of the two samples show that they can be decomposed into NbâO bonds at 530.9&nbsp;eV, CâO bonds at 532.0&nbsp;eV and CâO bonds at 533.5&nbsp;eV, separately. The Nb 3d-resolved spectra of the two samples show that both samples can be decomposed into four peaks (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d</a>, Supporting Information). For the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample, 3d<sub>5/2</sub> located at 207.9&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 210.6&nbsp;eV indicate the existence of Nb<sup>5+</sup>, while 3d<sub>5/2</sub> centered at 206.4&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 209.2&nbsp;eV specify the presence of Nb<sup>4+</sup>. In addition, for T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C samples, 3d<sub>5/2</sub> â207.9&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 210.7&nbsp;eV indicate the existence of Nb<sup>5+</sup>, while 3d<sub>5/2</sub> located at 206.6&nbsp;eV and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 209.3&nbsp;eV infer the presence of Nb<sup>4+</sup>. Compared to the 2.59% (at) of Nb<sup>4+</sup> in P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sample, the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C sample shows 2.90% (at) of Nb<sup>4+</sup>, indicating that the introduction of oxygen vacancies is beneficial to increase the Nb<sup>4+</sup> content, which in turn improves the electronic structure of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> material. Unlike P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C delivers the lowest Nb<sup>4+</sup> content of 2.33% (at), which may originate from the doping of the Se element.</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202312905-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The rate performance of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0006">6a,b</a>. The reversible specific capacity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> (0.25&nbsp;C) is 249&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, which is much higher than that of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (220&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>) and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C (208&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>). When the current density is increased to 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, the discharge capacities of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C can reach 214, 185, 148, 116, and 89&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, respectively. Even when the current density is further increased to 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> (25&nbsp;C), a discharge capacity of 63&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> can be achieved, corresponding to a charge and discharge time of only 46&nbsp;s. For comparison, the specific capacities of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C are only 33 and 42&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at the current density of 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>. After charging and discharging at a high current density of 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, the reversible capacity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode quickly recovered to 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> when the current density returned to 0.1A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, indicating the good rate response and high reversibility of the as-obtained materials. In addition, the corresponding coulomb efficiency of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode remains very stable throughout the rate-performance test and is much better than that of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). In order to explain the excellent rate performance of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode, the reaction kinetics of the three electrodes were studied by using the EIS technique. The Nyquist diagram of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes obtained by EIS are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0006">6c</a>. Among them, the semicircle from the high frequency to the middle-frequency region is related to the charge transfer reaction occurring at the electrolyte/electrode interface, which can be represented by the charge transfer resistance R<sub>ct</sub>, while the slash line in the low-frequency region can be ascribed to the diffusion of sodium ions inside the electrode, which can be represented by the Warburg impedance (W<sub>1</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0022_R_d17793163e2151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the equivalent circuit model of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), relevant parameters of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes were fitted, and the results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that, compared with P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode delivers the smallest Rct value (951Î©), indicating the best charge transfer reaction kinetics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/392cea33-24cb-4573-8e40-7bd7ee77f66c/adfm202312905-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/392cea33-24cb-4573-8e40-7bd7ee77f66c/adfm202312905-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95ece00a-1ff4-4937-8818-f9b2a91a5ad3/adfm202312905-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/392cea33-24cb-4573-8e40-7bd7ee77f66c/adfm202312905-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Rate performance, c) EIS curves (first cycle) of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes. d) Cycling performance of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>; e) Cycling performance of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cyclic performance of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes at 1.0&nbsp;Ag<sup>â1</sup> is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0006">6d</a>. It can be found that after 500 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, the capacity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode is still as high as 95&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, which is higher than that of P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (64&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>) and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrode (82&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>). Besides, a capacity of 107&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> can be achieved for T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode after 500 cycles under the current density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0006">6e</a>), corresponding to a capacity retention rate of 73.0%, indicating that T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode has good cyclic stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202312905-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Reversibility and Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The cyclic voltammetry curves of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>â1</sup> are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,b</a> (Supporting Information). On the first cathodic scan, the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrode shows a peak located at 1.0&nbsp;V, which is a typical cyclic voltammetric curve for sodium storage in T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and the first non-coincidence phenomenon can be attributed to the formation of SEI on the electrode surface and irreversible side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0002_R_d17793163e2357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0014_R_d17793163e2357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the first cycle, the CV curves of the last two turns of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrodes display a good overlap, indicating a good electrochemical reversibility. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a> (Supporting Information), the CV curves of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>â1</sup> were significantly different from the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrodes. At the first cathodic scan, the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode showed three peaks located at 1.0, 1.8, and 2.2&nbsp;V. The reduction peak located at 1.0&nbsp;V represents the formation of the SEI on the electrode surface and the irreversible side reactions, which is consistent with the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C electrodes. The reduction peaks located at 1.8 and 2.2&nbsp;V may be related to the first insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in NbSe<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0018_R_d17793163e2411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent two turns, the CV curve of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode shows the highest overlap, indicating the best electrochemical reversibility. In addition, it can be found in the subsequent two cycles that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode shows four pairs of well-defined redox peaks located at 1.29/1.42, 1.85/1.89, 1.93/2.08, and 2.00/2.16&nbsp;V, which can be ascribed to the reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> in NbSe<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0018_R_d17793163e2434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The charge/discharge profiles of the P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, and T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11dâf</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The discharge/charge capacity of the first cycle in P-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is 494/204&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, which corresponds to a coulombic efficiency of 41.3%. In the following cycle, it delivers a reversible capacity of â220&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>. For T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>@C, it shows a first charging capacity of 155&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> and a coulombic efficiency of 37.8%. As a comparison, the first discharge/charge capacity of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C is 535/242&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, corresponding a coulombic efficiency of 45.2%. Moreover, it still maintains a capacity of 249&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> in the next two cycles. The T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode delivers the highest capacity and coulombic efficiency compared to the other electrodes, indicating the best electrochemical reversibility. In addition, it is noted that there is a tiny plateau near 2.0&nbsp;V in T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode, corresponding to the reversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in NbSe<sub>2</sub> for the delocalization reaction, which is consistent with the CV curve. Besides, the electrochemical performance comparison of this material T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C with other metal oxides and hard carbon materials has been listed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C deliver relatively good electrochemical performance compared with inserted type metal oxides and hard carbon.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Subsequently, we investigated the sodium storage mechanism of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C through the in situ XRD (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202312905-fig-0007">7</a>). It can be observed that there are three distinct peaks during the charging and discharging process, which can be attributed to the (001), (180), and (200) diffraction peaks of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. When the battery was gradually discharged from the open-circuit potential to 3.0&nbsp;V, these three main peaks slowly moved to a lower angle and only slightly weakened in intensity. This indicates that Na<sup>+</sup> is reversibly inserted in the exfoliated T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> lattice. Meanwhile, it is clearly demonstrated that the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode conforms to the insertion/extraction mechanism. In addition, there are no correlation peaks of NbSe<sub>2</sub> were observed due to the weak peak intensity of NbSe<sub>2</sub> in the in situ XRD process. According to the previous reports,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202312905-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202312905-bib-0023_R_d17793163e2557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ChemistrySelect Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we know that Na<sup>+</sup> can be reversibly inserted/extracted from the NbSe<sub>2</sub> lattice, which belongs to the insertion/extraction mechanism. Specifically, the reaction mechanism can be expressed as follows:

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202312905-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20f1be55-3bd7-4b3a-9b59-7cffcd873df0/adfm202312905-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20f1be55-3bd7-4b3a-9b59-7cffcd873df0/adfm202312905-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/46b71fda-915a-4001-805b-dedfeaf5c7b0/adfm202312905-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20f1be55-3bd7-4b3a-9b59-7cffcd873df0/adfm202312905-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202312905-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202312905" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode during the first charge/discharge process; b) Corresponding intensity contour maps concerning the evolution of the main characteristic diffraction peaks of (001), (180) and (200).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In conclusion, the Na<sup>+</sup> can be highly reversible inserted/extracted in the T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C electrode during the charging and discharging process, which is consistent with the insertion/extraction mechanism.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202312905-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202312905-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, the conductor heterostructure Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub> material was successfully obtained by an in situ selenization process, which not only significantly improves the electronic conductivity of the material, but also optimizes the crystal structure of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, efficiently promoting the delocalization of electron. The inserted energy of Na<sup>+</sup> was largely decreased from 9.88 to â3.54&nbsp;eV, as well as greatly improving the electrical conductivity of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> from 1.87&nbsp;Ã&nbsp;10<sup>â4</sup> to 3.69&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>â1</sup>. Remarkably, the as-fabricated T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C delivers an impressive reversible capacity of 249&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup>, and an excellent rate performance of 63&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> at 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>â1</sup> due to the introduction of the conducting phase NbSe<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the in situ XRD results clearly indicate that the sodium storage mechanism of T-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5-x</sub>-NbSe<sub>2</sub>@C is an insertion/extraction reaction through the evolution law of characteristic peaks. This work provides an innovative routine to modify the conductivity of materials by constructing conductor heterojunction, which may be expanded to other electrode materials with poor electronic conductivity.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
